The other interpretation of ubdy 'uncultivated (land)' derives the term from 'bd , to perish, be lost'. Some new support for this rendering comes from 2031 in which s^d ubdy (: 3, 4, 5, 8, 10) is contrasted with s^d bCly (: 7) , Bacl (= fertile) land' (cf. AB* designating rain-fed land, as distinct from *AB*I*AB* designating unproductive land; both terms hark back to bcl and mt of ancient Canaan). On the other hand, the subject of so many U garitic land grants ought to be good, rather th an bad, land; ubdy should therefore mean ' perpetual land grant', anll perhaps also the ' grantee ' to whom such land is given. Text 1081 : 7, 9 associates the term with krm, which rules out ' uncultiva· ted '. The construct pl. krm in (1081 : 9) t_lt_ . krm . ubdym (contrast the absolute pl. krmm in s^bc • krmm , 7 vineyards' in line 25) suggests the translation ' 3 vineyards of grantees'. (§ 5.17). ln any case, the compound is treated as a single word, as the nom. ending in bunu-s^'lt inùicates. 1037. t*ll 'to fan (of dew)' (1 Aqht: 41); t*l 'dew' (6: 27; 1 Aqht:44, 51, 55, 200: cnt: II : 39, 40); t*l mrt_ (124 : 20) , wine-dew' if the word-division is right; tly bt rb (51: l : 18, IV : 56, VI: 11; 67: V : 11; cnt : l : 24-25, III : 4): n. of one of Bacl's daughters (§§ 5. 42; 8. 54) to be vocalized after 't*a-la-ia (PRU III, p. 258). l~, ~, jb, mA. The fact that Ba <l, as a god of fertilitygiving water, is associated with dew as well as rain in 1 Aqht: 44, rules out any seasonal interpretation of his role (cf. Cassuto, IEJ 12 1962 77-86; Gordon, Orientalia 22 1953 79-81). Dew in Canaan is a round-the-year phe. nomenon. It condenses most when the rainfall is least (see M. Gilead and N. Rosenan, 'Ten Years of Dew Observation in Israel', IEJ 4 1954 120-3). Dew is connected with fertility (e. g., Gen. 27: 28 and cnt:II:39 cited in § 17.4) qui te like rain. For the concept that dew in summer takes the place of rain in winter, note the J ewish prayer for dew which states l~ cwlttt:1 aWbW Cttt;J cwltttl kl 'depart in peace, 0 rain, and come in peace, o dew?' anticipating the end of 

- 406 - 

the rainy winter and start of the dewy summer. Much of what has been mistaken for seasonal pattern in Ug. mythology, is in fact sabbatical cycle, as l first pointed out in UL 3-5; see now my World of O. T. 98-99.
